Problem Statement ^psOt4fow

Market Opportunity ^O48XyQwr

>> Censorship.
    >> Content moderation expenses - Platforms spend billions annually on moderation infrastructure.
    >> Compliance costs - Companies must comply with laws like the EU Digital Services Act, incurring millions in legal and operational costs.
        >> Unenforceable to any decentralized system.
        >> Not within the jurisdiction of any country.
    >> Lost advertising revenue - Content creators and platforms lose ad dollars due to demonetization or brand safety restrictions.
    >> Reduced market entry - Startups face high moderation/compliance burdens, discouraging innovation and increasing barriers to entry.
    >> Litigation and legal risk - Defending moderation decisions or facing lawsuits for content (especially in the EU) costs companies millions per year.
    >> Economic impact of deplatforming - Influencers or creators banned from platforms can lose substantial income streams, sometimes amounting to hundreds of thousands annually.
    >> Reduced productivity - Over-censorship in workspaces or academia can delay projects, research, or collaboration.
    >> Innovation slowdown - Suppression of controversial research, art, or political discourse can reduce long-term economic value creation.
    >> Chilling effect on speech - People self-censor out of fear of backlash, career risk, or public shaming.
    >> Polarization and distrust - Perceived bias in censorship decisions fosters political alienation and undermines trust in institutions and media.
    >> Fractured discourse - Banned or censored voices move to fringe platforms, creating echo chambers and extremism.
    >> Erosion of democratic values - Overreach in moderation or legal enforcement weakens the norms of free expression and open debate.
    >> Cultural homogenization - Controversial or minority viewpoints may be suppressed, leading to less diversity of thought.
    >> Suppression of dissent - Activists, whistle-blowers, or minority voices may be disproportionately affected, undermining social progress.
    >> Reduced academic freedom - Scholars may avoid politically sensitive topics, damaging knowledge production and intellectual rigor.
    >> Public fear and anxiety - The knowledge that one's words are being monitored or judged can create a constant background of social stress.

        >> Shareholder impact.
            >> Stock volatility - Censorship-related controversies can cause significant drops in share value.
            >> Ad revenue loss - Companies can be deplatformed, demonetized, or lose major advertisers if their content or messaging is censored or controversial.
            >> Regulatory costs - Legal battles over speech rights drain resources and increase operational costs.
            >> Brand trust erosion - Perceived bias or censorship can make a company look partisan or untrustworthy, driving away both users and investors.
            >> Investor backlash - ESG or value-driven investors may divest if a company is seen as either too restrictive (limiting free expression) or not restrictive enough.
            >> Reduced platform engagement - Over-censorship can lead to user disengagement, reducing active user base metrics, critical for valuation.

        >> Stakeholder impact.
            >> Employee resignation or whistleblowing - Ethical conflicts over internal censorship policies can lead to loss of talent or public leaks.
            >> Customer churn - Users may leave platforms perceived as too censorious or too permissive, impacting growth and profitability.
            >> Market segmentation - Censorship can alienate one politically or cultural group, forcing businesses to "pick sides" and lose half the market.
            >> Loss of credibility - Being seen as suppressing ideas or voices damages the company's moral authority and brand voice.
            >> Distrust in leadership - Stakeholders may question whether leadership prioritizes political correctness over integrity or vice versa.
            >> Fractures community - Censorship can create divisions among users, customers, or employees weakening loyalty and cohesion.

        >> Executive impact.
            >> Legal liability - Poorly applied censorship may result in lawsuits or regulatory investigations.
            >> Lost business opportunities - Partnerships may collapse if a company is seen as too controversial or politically unpalatable due to censorship issues.
            >> Operational costs - Running content moderation teams, AI filters, appeals processes, and compliance with speech laws can cost millions annually.
            >> Reputational tightrope - Founders walk a fine line between free speech advocacy and responsible platform management, with few ways to satisfy both extremes.
            >> Inhibited innovation - Overfly cautious internal culture can stifle experimentation, especially in media, tech, and academia.
            >> Cultural stagnation - Censorship of "risky" ideas reduces creative freedoms, affecting advertising, product design, and brand identity.

        >> User impact.
            >> Loss of access to information - Users may be blocked from vital health, political, or economic content during elections or public crises.
            >> Platform bans - Content creators or users can lose income streams without due process.
            >> Reduced service utility - Content filters and censorship can degrade user experience, making platforms less useful or trustworthy.
            >> Forced migration to less secure platforms - Censored users often move to less regulated or fringe platforms, increasing risk of data abuse or scams.
            >> Higher data risk via opaque moderation - Censorship algorithms require deep access to user data and behavior, increasing surveillance and breach risk.
            >> Chilling effect on free expression - Users may self-censor online to avoid bans, ridicule, or algorithmic suppression, leading to loss of authentic speech.
            >> Erosion of trust in platforms - Perceived political or cultural bias in moderation reduces confidence in digital ecosystem.
            >> Social isolation / digital exclusion - Deplatformed or marginalized users may lose communities or social support networks built online.
            >> Loss of identity / reputation - Content removal or censorship can erase parts of a user's digital identity.
            >> Data misuse and resale - Corrupt handling of personal data can lead to identity theft, spam, or financial fraud.
            >> Increased surveillance exposure - Collusion between corporations and governments puts users under constant surveillance.
            >> Discriminatory service delivery - Corrupted or biased algorithms may influence credit decisions, job offers, or housing access.
            >> Lack of resource - Users may have no clear path to report or reverse data abuse and complaints are ignored or covered up.
            >> Loss of privacy - Once users realize their data is being exploited without consent, it creates paranoia and resignation "digital fatalism".
            >> Powerlessness - In environments where institutions collude or hide misconduct, users feel they can't protect their rights, even if laws exist.
            >> Loss of digital dignity - Users may feel reduced to a data profile or behavioral score, rather than seen as human individuals.
            >> Erosion of civic trust - In public agencies or media platforms are corrupt or censorious, people may detach from democratic engagement, believing it's futile.

            >> Example.
                >> Facebook-Cambridge Analytica - Millions of users' data used for political manipulation without consent, erosion of platform trust.
                >> Apple iCloud China data hosting - User data of Chinese citizens stored under Chinese jurisdiction.
                >> Covid misinformation bans - Users where banned and censored for posting views later proven valid.
                >> EU GDPR violations - Companies fined billions, but users still rarely see data rights effectively enforced.
                >> TikTok data-sharing concerns - Users fear data exposure to authoritarian governments, leading to bans and migration to alternative platforms.
        
>> Corruption.
    >> Public funds misallocated - Corrupt contracts, inflated prices, and crony deals waste taxpayer money (ei. construction, defense, pharma).
    >> Reduced foreign investment - Investors avoid countries perceived as corrupt due to legal reputation risks.
    >> Market inefficiencies - Corruption distorts competition, creating monopolies or oligopolies.
    >> Increased cost of public goods - Corruption inflates the price of infrastructure, healthcare, and services.
    >> Tax evasion and fraud - Undermines state revenue, shifting burden to compliant taxpayers.
    >> Fines and settlements - Companies involved in bribery or fraud (ie. Siemens, Goldman Sachs) pay billions in penalties.
    >> Increased audit and oversight costs - Governments and companies must spend heavily on detection, compliance, and control systems.
    >> Erosion of public trust - People lose faith in institution, believing "the system is rigged". Decline in political participation, voter turnout, and civic engagement.
    >> Weakening of democratic institutions - Corruption undermines judicial independence, media freedom, and rule of law. Leads to regulatory capture, where laws benefit the few rather than the many.
    >> Undermining meritocracy - Jobs, promotions, and contracts go to the well-connected, not the most qualified. Young talent emigrates or disengages due to lack of opportunity.
    >> Perpetuation of inequality - Corruption disproportionately harms lower-income groups who rely more on public services. Wealthy individuals and corporations can bypass laws or manipulate them.
    >> Normalization of unethical behavior -  "Everyone's doing it" mentality spreads in politics, business, and even academia. Cultural tolerance for small-scale corruption (ie. nepotism, insider favors).
    >> Policy distortion - Decisions are made based on donor influence or lobbying power, not public interest (ie. Big Oil, military-industrial complex).

    >> Shareholder impact.
        >> Stock value volatility - Corruption scandals can cause immediate drops in stock price and long-term reputational damage.
        >> Fines and penalties - Companies like Siemens, Volkswagen, and Goldman Sachs have paid billions due to regulatory violations tied to corrupt practices.
        >> Dividends affected - Legal and settlement costs eat into profits, reducing dividend payouts or share buybacks.
        >> Reputational damage - Long-term brand perception is harmed, reducing customer loyalty and investor confidence.
        >> Governance concerns - Corruption signals weak internal controls and poor board oversight, which can deter institutional investors.
        >> Decreased investor trust - Investors may discount valuation multiples due to governance risk premiums.
    
    >> Stakeholder impact.
        >> Reduced employee morale - Workers lose motivation when promotions, bonuses, or contracts are based on favoritism or bribery.
        >> Lost business opportunities - Stakeholders may be excluded from supply chains or public contracts without connections.
        >> Legal exposure - Stakeholders involved in projects under investigation may face indirect legal or financial risks.
        >> Erosion of internal culture - A corrupt or secretive environment breeds cynicism, distrust, and internal conflict.
        >> Customer backlash - Consumers today are increasingly values-driven; perceived corruption or censorship leads to boycotts or negative press.
        >> Reduced community trust - Firms seen as unethical lose their "license to operate" in local communities.

    >> Executive impact.
        >> Higher operational costs - Bribes, lobbying, and "relationship management" add hidden expenses.
        >> Unfair competition - Honest firms lose out to corrupt competitors who can undercut pricing via unethical means.
        >> Compliance burdens - Must invest heavily in compliance, legal, and audit systems to meet anti-corruption laws.
        >> Strategic misalignment - Time and resources are diverted away from innovation and growth towards damage control or political maneuvering.
        >> Censorship pressures - Executives face pressure to conform to political or social norms, limiting authentic leadership and public engagement.
        >> Global reputational risk - Corruption or speed scandals can derail international expansion, partnerships, or IPOs.

>> Adoption barrier.
    >> False decentralization - Many so-called decentralized apps (dApps) rely on centralized backends or dev team.
    >> Ideological polarization - Crypto and blockchain communities can sometimes become echo chambers, making balanced debate difficult.
    >> Regulatory uncertainty - Governments in the US, EU, and elsewhere are cracking down on privacy coins, self-custody wallets, and DeFi platforms. Ambiguity around what constitute a security or illegal fundraising mechanism.
    >> Corruption in blockchain ecosystem - Projects misuse funds. Projects claim to be decentralized but are controlled by small dev teams or VC Whales.
    >> Censorship by infrastructure - Cloud providers, domain registrars, and frontend hosts can be forced to comply with censorship laws. NFT platforms can delist content. Web3 social platforms can still "shadowban" wallets or limit visibility.
    >> Legal and financial risk for users - Users in regulated regions may face taxation, asset freezes, or legal consequences for participating in certain DAOs, using mixers, or transacting with blacklisted wallets.
    >> Usability barriers - Self-custody wallets, private key management, and blockchain UX are still too complex for average users, limiting adoption and increasing accidental data loss.

    >> Transaction-based payment model limitations - Current blockchain architecture relies on per-transaction fees and digital signatures, which creates major friction for continuous or real-time interaction.
        >> Streaming content (video, audio, live data) is not viable natively onchain, as each interaction would require user signatures and fee payments.
        >> Users must pay gas fees even for minor actions, hindering interactivity and engagement.
        >> Most dApps offload real-time functionality to centralized infrastructure, undermining decentralized goals.
        >> Layer 2 and micro-payment solutions exist but remain complex and under-adopted.

    >> Poor developer experience - Building dApps remains far more complex than Web2.
        >> Fragmented tooling, lack of standardization across chains (Solidity vs. Move vs. Rust).
        >> Devs must understand wallet integration, onchina/offchain logic, gas optimization, and more.
        >> Bugs in smart contracts are costly and irreversible, increasing risk for both developers and users.
    
    >> Fragmentation of ecosystems - The multichain world creates silos, friction, and user confusion.
        >> Users must constantly bridge assets between chains, increasing risk of loss.
        >> Cross-chain compatibility is still immature, with bridges frequently hacked or failing.
        >> App availability and liquidity are unevenly distributed across chains.

    >> Security vulnerabilities and smart contract risk - Hacks, exploits, and vulnerabilities continue to plague the cosystem.
        >> Billions have been lost to reentrancy attacks, rug pulls, flash loads exploits, and buggy protocols.
        >> Insurance solutions exists but are fragmented and limited.
        >> Users are often not equipped to audit or verify the safety of a contract.

    >> User identity and recovery issues - There's still no good solution for lost keys or account recovery.
        >> Lost wallet are lost assets permanently.
        >> Social recovery and account abstraction are in development but not yet widely implemented.
        >> Managing multiple wallets across chains is confusing and error-prone.

    >> Lack of real-world utility - Outside of speculation, NFTs, and DeFi, many blockchain applications still lack mainstream relevant.
        >> Real-world integrations remain experimental or siloed.
        >> Enterprise adoption is slow due to compliance, cost, and ROI concerns.

 ^X0w3mWQN

>> The web3 market has 40 - 45% CAGR.
>> The web3 market has a TVL of $160B. ^eKolDqWa

>> Retailer - Buy finished goods from manufacturers or distributors and interface directly with customers.
>> Manufacturer - Make custom goods or highly replicated, mass-produced products and can sell what it makes to distributors, retailers, or directly to customers.
>> Fee-for-Service - Charge an hourly rate or a fixed cost for a specific agreement.
>> Subscription - A product that requires ongoing payment, usually in return for a fixed duration.
>> Freemium - Attract customers by introducing them to basic, limited-scope products.
>> Bundling - Incentive by offering pricing discounts for buying multiple products.
>> Marketplace - Receive compensation for hosting a platform for business to be conducted.
>> Franchise - Leverages existing business plans to expand and reproduce a company at a different location.
>> Pay-as-You-Go - Charge a fixed fee for offering the service in addition to an amount that changes each month based on what was consumed.
>> Brokerage - Connect buyers and sellers without directly selling a good themselves.
>> Affiliate - Pay an entity to promote a good, and that entity often receives compensation in exchange for its promotion. ^VYEJwC34

>> Direct Democracy - Members make laws and policy decisions themselves, often through referendums and initiatives.
>> Representative Democracy - Members elect representatives who make decisions on their behalf.
    >> Parliamentary System - Executive (Prime Minister + Cabinet) emerges from legislature and is accountable to it.
    >> Presidential System - Clear separation of powers between executive, legislature, and judiciary.
    >> Semi-Presidential System - Dual executive – a president (usually directly elected) shares power with a prime minister.
>> Federal vs. Unitary Democracies - Power is constitutionally divided between central and regional governments.
 ^an50ZJIT

Election: By Voting Method ^93s9te7z

>> First-Past-the-Post - Candidate with the most votes wins.
>> Majoritarian - Candidate must secure more than half of votes.
>> Proportional Representation - Seats are distributed based on the proportion of votes each party receives.

Week 1 ^4IrC2B6R

>> The DAO Development team started on the 18th of August.

    >> Revenue.

    >> Token sales.
    >> Yield.

    >> Monthly burn rate (avg. expense per month).
    >> Treasury runway (how long current funds will last at current burn).
    >> Committed vs. unallocated funds.

>> Asset health and risk.
    >> Stablecoin % vs. volatile assets (treasury risk exposure).
    >> Liquidity of assets.
    >> Yield-bearing positions (e.g staking LP tokens, lending positions).

>> Governance and allocations.
    >> Active funding proposals pending (and amounts requested).
    >> Historical treasury utilization.

>> Metrics

>> Total supply.
>> Circulating supply.
>> Emission schedule.
>> Distribution.

>> Value capture and sustainability.
    >> Revenue flow (how does value accrue to token holders through fees, buybacks, dividends, staking yield, etc.?).
    >> Treasury backing (assets in the treasury supporting token value).
    >> Burn vs. mint dynamics (how token scarcity or inflation is managed).
    >> Token velocity controls (mechanisms to discourage speculation).

>> Governance
    >> Voting power mechanic (1 token = 1 vote, quadratic voting, delegation, reputation-weighted, etc.).
    >> Proposal threshold (minimum tokens needed to propose/vote).
    >> Treasury influence (how does the treasury affect governance).
    >> Sybil resistance (how to prevent governance manipulation).

Code
    >> Reentrancy attack. -- The DAO Hack (2016)
    >> Integer overflow/underflow.
    >> Unprotected self-destruct or upgrade function.
    >> Front-running and MEV (miner/validator extractable value).
        >> Curve Finance / YFI votes
            >> Opportunistic actors front-ran governance proposals and vote submissions to manipulate outcomes.
            >> Not always full exploits, but MEV bots can distort DAO fairness.

Governance
    >> Majority attack or governance takeover.
    >> Flash loan governance attacks. | Beanstalk DAO, April 2022
    >> Sybil attacks.
    >> Low voter participation.
        >> Many DAOs (including Compound, Uniswap) see <5% token participation.
        >> Makes governance easy to hijack by whales or coordinated groups.

>> Operational.
    >> Poor proposal validation.
    >> Treasury risks.
    >> Oracle manipulation.
    >> Governance fatigue.

Fundraising ^JFHMydIG

>> Bootstrap - Demonstrate traction with your own resources or revenue before seeking external funding.
>> Angel investors - Target individuals who understand your industry. They often provide valuable mentorship alongside capital. Use platforms like AngelList or leverage warm introductions.
>> Accelerators - Programs like Y Combinator, Techstars, or industry-specific accelerators provide funding plus structured support and network access.
>> Venture capital - Research firms that invest in your sector and stage. Look at their portfolio companies and investment thesis. Warm introductions through mutual connections work best.
>> Strategic investors - Corporations in your industry can provide funding plus partnership opportunities and market access.
>> Revenue-based financing - Good for businesses with predictable revenue streams. You repay based on monthly revenue percentage rather than equity dilution.
>> Crowdfunding - Platforms like Kickstarter (product-based) or equity crowdfunding for community-driven businesses.
>> Grants - Government programs, foundation grants, or industry-specific funding for certain sectors. ^8q95A7BF

    pub type Result<T> = ::core::result::Result<T, Error>;
    
    pub enum Error {
        
    }

    pub trait Proposal {
        fn name(&self) -> Utf8<32>;
        fn description(&self) -> Utf8<256>;
        fn quorum(&self) -> u128;
        fn time_left(&self) -> u64;
        fn timelock(&self) -> u64;
        fn support(&mut self) -> Result<()>;
        fn abstain(&mut self) -> Result<()>;
        fn reject(&mut self) -> Result<()>;
    }
} ^6er1oZve

    pub type Result<T> = ::core::result::Result<T, Error>;
    
    pub enum Error {
        
    }
    
    pub trait Kernel {
        fn install(&mut self, package_name: Utf8<32>, version: u32);
        fn uninstall(&mut self, package_name: Utf8<32>);
        fn load(&self, key: [u8; 32]) -> Bytes;
        fn save(&mut self, key: [u8; 32], bytes: Bytes) -> Result<()>;
    }
} ^cHzN34Sp

>> `refine()`.
    >> Accepts up to 5mb of data.
    >> Returns up to 4kb of data.
    >> May execute for up to 6 seconds of PVM gas.
    >> Has data about work package.

>> `accumulate()`.
    >> Accepts multiple outputs from refine.
    >> May execute for up to 10ms of PVM gas per output.
    >> Stateful - May transfer funds, alter state, read other service's state.
    >> May create services, and upgrade its code.

>> `on_transfer()`.
    >> Stateful - May alter state and read other service's state. ^5EYCQ8MY

mod jam_service {
    use ::alloc::vec;
    use ::jam_pvm_common as jam;
    use ::jam_types::*;
    
    jam::declare_service!(LightBulb);

    #[repr(u8)]
    #[derive(Clone)]
    #[derive(Copy)]
    #[derive(Default)]
    pub enum Toggle {
        #[default]    
        Off,
        On
    }

    pub struct LightBulb {
        toggle: Toggle
    }

    impl jam::Service for LightBulb {
        fn refine(
            id: ServiceId, 
            payload: WorkPayload, 
            package_hash: WorkPackageHash, 
            content: RefineContext, 
            auth_code_hash: CodeHash
        ) -> WorkOutput {
            let payload = payload.take();
            if payload == b"toggle" {
                b"toggle".to_vec().into()
            } else {
                vec::Vec::new().into()
            }
        }

        fn accumulate(slot: Slot, id: ServiceId, items: V) -> Option<Hash> {
            
        }

        fn on_transfer(slot: Slot, id: ServiceId, items: Vec<TransferRecord>) {

        }
    }
} ^BCiKdJDC

Proposal ^hs8ljjUA

* `history`: Historical quorum %'s.
* `cliff`: Steepness control for quorum curve.
* `min_percent`: Minimum quorum % allowed.
* `max_percent`: Maximum quorum % allowed.

fn quorum(
    history: Vec<f64>,
    optimal: f64,
    cliff: f64,
    min_percent: f64,
    max_percent: f64
) -> f64 {
    let history: Vec<f64> = history.iter().map(|&percentage| {
        percentage / 100.0
    }).collect();
    let average_participation = history.iter().copied().sum::<f64>();
    let min_quorum: f64 = min_percent / 100.0;
    let max_quorum: f64 = max_percent / 100.0;
    let center_l: f64 = min_quorum + (max_quorum - min_quorum) * 0.3;
    let center_r: f64 = min_quorum + (max_quorum - min_quorum) * 0.7;
    let l: f64 = 1.0 / (1.0 + (-cliff * (average_participation - center_l)).exp());
    let r: f64 = 1.0 / (1.0 + (cliff * (average_participation - center_r)).exp());
    let ret: f64 = l * r;
    ret
} ^A2G6oOMn

fn csc(
    x: f64,
    c: f64,
    m_0: f64,
    m_1: f64,
    k: f64,
    a: f64
) -> f64 {
    let c_l: f64 = m_0 + a (c - m_0);
    let c_r: f64 = c + a * (m_1 - c);
    let l: f64 = sigm(k * (x - c_l));
    let r: f64 = sigm(-k * (x - c_r));
    let ret: f64 = l * r;
    ret
}



fn sigm(n: f64) -> f64 {
    1.0 / (1.0 + (-n).exp())
} ^qwhrEN8b

Value Proposition ^Kx6bjyYb

>> Tech Stack

>> Competitive advantages of building in Rust (vs Solidity or non-Rust platforms).
    
    >> Superior safety and reliability - Rust offers memory safety without garbage collection or manual memory management.
        >> Reduces attack surface (buffer overflows, null pointer errors).
        >> Eliminates whole classes of runtime bugs common in Solidity and JavaScript-based chains.
    
        >> Strong static typing and compile time checks.
            >> Prevents logic errors before deployment.
            >> More secure by design compared to Solidity's dynamic typing.

    >> System-level flexibility - Custom runtimes possible in Rust (vs fixed VM in Solidity).
        >> No need to conform to EVM opcodes or limitations.
        >> Developers can build tailored execution environments for specific use cases.
        
        >> Supports async logic and multithreading.
            >> Enabled use cases like real-time collaboration, gaming, or data streaming.
            >> Solidity and EVM are fundamentally synchronous, blocking execution models limit innovation.

    >> Performance and scalability.
        >> Rust compiles to high-performance WASM (used in Polkadot JAM). With lower latency, faster execution, and deterministic behavior.
        >> Faster runtimes mean cheaper operations. JAM-style chains can offer much lower transaction fees vs EVM chains.
        >> Parallelism-ready architecture. Unlike Solidity's single-threaded model, Rust-based chains can support parallel execution and async processing.

    >> Developer experience and ecosystem quality.
        >> Modern developer tooling and IDE support. Rust has best-in-class tooling (cargo, clippy, rust analyzer).
        >> Growing open-source ecosystem. Shared libraries (cryptography, networking, WASM) are actively maintained and production-grade.
        
        >> Stronger engineering talent pool.
            >> Rust developers tend to be experienced systems engineers.
            >> Harder to hire solidity developers, and many Solidity developers lack relevant experience.

    >> Governance and upgradability.
        >> Rust-based chains like Polkadot support native onchain governance.
            >> More robust upgrade mechanisms than most Solidity chains.
            >> Avoids the risk of proxy patterns and delegate calls common in Solidity upgrades.
        
    >> Interoperability and composability.
        >> Polkadot JAM offers async cross-chain messaging XCM. Rust native parachains can interoperate without relying on fragile bridges.
        >> Most EVM chains are isolated unless bridged. Bridges introduce security vulnerabilities (Harmony, Ronin, Wormhole hacks).
        >> Rust ecosystem can interconnect or influence shared standards.

    >> First-mover advantage in Rust-native web3.
        >> Most web3 projects are still Solidity/EVM-based. Rust-native services are underrepresented despite better architecture.
        >> Being early in the JAM ecosystem or Polkadot 2.0 positions us positively.
            >> As an infrastructure leader rather than another dApp.
            >> To capture developer migration from Web2 systems engineering to Web3.






>> Rust vs. Solidity.
    >> Solidity is still the dominant language for smart contract develoment (EVM, Ethereum).
    
    >> Solidity's core limitations - Solidity is ideal for DeFi 1.0 and simple contracts, not full-scale decentralized systems.
        >> Poor scalability - Monolithic execution layers can't handle complex dApps.
        >> Gas cost unpredictability - Friction for both developers and users.
        >> Security vulnerabilities - Weak typing leads to runtime errors and exploits.
        >> Limited async support - Streaming, real-time logic is nearly impossible natively.
        >> Tooling fragmentation - Debugging, testing, and optimization are still immature.
       
    >> Rust.
        >> Custom runtimes - Full control over execution logic.
        >> Asynchronous composability - Support for real-time coordination and data flow.
        >> Shared security - No need to bootstrap own validator set.
        
   >> Infrastructure-layer innovation.
   ^lvEUSplE

    // member management
    // ownership management
    // hierarchy authentication
    // events, proposals with cohort of members
    // schedule payments and manage payments -> payment??
    // treasury pool/vault ^fDmCxva0

Polkadot JAM ^eYceqKss

>> A new protocol to replace Polkadot's current RelayChain.
 ^yl19wRBM

PolkaVM ^8lhq5hnD

>> A custom VM built on a RISC-V architecture, using a register-based model (rather than Ethereum's stack-based EVM).
>> It supports Solidity (and other high-performance languages via LLVM).
>> It integrates with the Polkadot runtime.
>> Hosted inside JAM using FRAME pallet. ^Jyr2vbTJ

Substrate ^X2b0t054

>> A modular blockchain framework developed by Parity Technologies that allows developers to build custom blockchains.

>> Features.
    >> Written in Rust.
    >> Highly modular.
    >> Can be used to build standalone blockchains or parachains for Polkadot and Kusama.
    >> Supports WASM-based runtime logic, enabling upgradable chains without hard forks.
     ^LazIf8is

Substrate FRAME (Framework for Runtime Aggregation of Modularized Entities) ^pakMvxxM

>> A collection of Rust libraries and macros that make it easy to build the runtime (state transition logic) of a Substrate chain.
>> Pallet - Module that defines parts of the blockchain's functionality.
>> Runtime - Actual logic of the blockchain, built from pallets.
>> Dispatchables - Functions users can call (extrinsics).
>> Storage - Key-value data storage (mapped to onchain state).
>> Event and errors - Used for feedback and traceability.

>> Development flow
    >> Select or build pallets using FRAME (ie. balances, governance, smart contracts).
    >> Compose them into a runtime.
    >> Compile the runtime to WASM.
    >> Run your node, optionally connect it to Polkadot as a parachain. ^sDyqBK8c

JAM ^rQRzXJAx

>> Built with Substrate.
>> Uses Substrate FRAME to build its runtime and support multiple VMs. ^zNRRKpzd

Service ^RkLe3bRq

>> A PVM executable.
>> Can be written in any language that compiles to RISC-V.
>> Is a ".bin" and "metadata.json".
>> Can be deployed directly to Polkadot (JAM previously RelayChain).

>> Deployment.
    >> Register on a JAM chain (ie. JAM-enabled Asset Hub or JAM Core).
    >> Called/invoked by users, scheduled for execution.
    >> Charged gas / compute time like a smart contract.
    >> Executed deterministically by PVM nodes.
 ^s5hDH7HI

Coretime ^RBxHffO0

Scheduler ^grb82R7a

>> Rented compute time on Polkadot Validators.
>> Required to run JAM services.
>> Replaces parachain slots in the JAM model.

>> Bulk.
    >> Reserved in advance (predictable, long-term).
    >> Like renting a server monthly.

>> Instant.
    >> On-demand, spot-market compute.
    >> Like AWS lambda - pay per execution.

>> Advantages over Ethereum or Solana.

    >> Modular, verifiable offchain compute.
        >> Allows trusted execution of complex offchain logic with cryptographic proofs.
        >> Services can run arbitrary RISC-V code offchain but prove correctness onchain.
        >> Ethereum and Solana mostly run logic directly onchain (EVM or BPF), limited by gas and block time.
        >> Enable heavy compute without onchain gas costs or limits, verified by lightweight proofs.

    >> Long-running, stateful background services.
        >> Supports persistent services that run continuously (like daemons or servers).
        >> Can maintain internal state and communicate via messages.
        >> Ethereum smart contracts are stateless between transactions, needing external triggers.
        >> Can support background AI, offchain indexing, real-time data aggregation.

    >> Low-latency, scalable, event-driven services.
        >> Services can subscribe to blockchain events and respond quickly offchain.
        >> Enables real-time or near-real-time game logic, market-making bots, oracles.
        >> Ethereum/Solana oracles are often third-party or centralized.
        >> Offers on-chain trust + offchain speed and scalability.

    >> Native support for complex, layered architectures.
        >> Promotes composable microservices that can be independently deployed and upgraded.
        >> Easier to build complex, modular dApps and games with clear service boundaries.
        >> Ethereum contracts are often monolithic or require complex proxy patterns.
        >> Solana programs are fast but less modular in deployment/upgrade strategy.

    >> Flexible compute pricing with coretime market.
        >> Uses a market-driven compute pricing for efficient resource allocation.
        >> Unlike Ethereum gas, pricing is dynamic and market-based, optimizing cost and availability.
        >> Encourages bulk purchase for predicable costs, beneficial for game developers and data-heavy applications.

    >> Better support for heavy data and state intensive applications.
        >> Can handle large amounts of scale and data indexing offchain verified onchain.
        >> Ideal for onchain analytics, indexing, AI/ML model serving, complex games.
        >> Ethereum and Solana face high gas costs and data limits onchain, limiting heavy workloads.

    >> Possible use cases that are not possible on other networks.
        >> Verified offchain game backends - High scale MMO logic running with verifiable proofs.
        >> Onchain verified AI inference - Heavy AI/ML tasks with integrity guarantees.
        >> Real-time DeFi market making bots - Reacting to live dataa with offchain speed and onchain trust.
        >> Cross-chain asset bridges with offchain relayers verified by cryptographic proofs.
        >> Large-scale decentralized oracles and data aggregators with built-in verifiability.
        >> Composable microservice ecosystems for decentralized social networks and market places.

>> Performance

    >> MMO with 10,000 to 100,000 moves per player per day.
        >> Cost per action (logic only, optimized) - ~$0.00001 to $0.00005.
        >> Player daily action count - 10,000 to 100,000 moves/interactions.

        >> Single player daily cost estimate.
            >> 10,000 actions * $0.00001 = $0.10/day.
            >> 10,000 actions * $0.00005 = $0.50/day.
            >> 100,000 actions * $0.00001 = $1.00/day.
            >> 100,000 actions * $0.00005 = $5.00/day.

        >> Scaling to 100,000 players.
            
            >> Mid-level activity (10,000 actions/player/day).
                >> $0.10/player/day - $10,000/day - ~$300,000/month.
                >> $0.50/player/day - $50,000/day - ~$1.5M/month.
        
            >> High activity (100,000 actions/player/day).
                >> $1.00/player/day - $100,000/day - ~$3M/month.
                >> $5.00/player/day - $500,000/day - ~$15M/month.
        
        >> Coretime can support large-scale MMOs technically.
        >> High-frequency on-chain actions still very expensive.
        
        >> Optimization.
            >> Batch actions client-side (ie. send 50 moves as 1 transaction).
            >> Sparse checkpoints - Only save critical game state.
            >> Player-funded execution - Player buy coretime for their actions.
            >> Persist only essential state - Minimize what's stored onchain.
            >> Shard MMO zones into separate JAM services.

    >> GTA-Style Game Logic (No Rendering).
        >> Player movement & position updates (continuous or frequent).
        >> Vehicle controls and states (location, speed, damage).
        >> Combat logic (shooting, damage calculation).
        >> Inventory management (weapons, items, health).
        >> Mission state and event triggers.
        >> NPC AI states (simplified, tick-based).
        >> Interactions with world objects (doors, pickups, shops).
        >> Multiplayer synchronization logic (state changes, chat metadata).

        >> Frequency and complexity.
            >> Updates every 1 - 5 seconds per player (no rendering but responsive logic).
            >> Each update - Position, actions, inventory, combat calculations.
            >> Instructions per update - Roughly 50,000 - 150,000 (complex logic).
            >> Tick-based simulation instead of continuous frame-by-frame.

        >> Cost estimate per player.
            >> Cost per instruction - ~$0.0000000001.
            >> Cost per update - 100,000 instructions * $0.0000000001 = $0.00001.
            >> Updates per minute - 12 (every 5 seconds).
            >> Cost per minute - 12 * $0.00001 = $0.00012.
            >> Cost per hour - $0.00012 * 60 = $0.0072 per hour per player.
            >> Cost per day (8 hours active) - $0.0072 * 8 = $0.0576 per day.

        >> Scaling to 100,000 players.
            >> Hourly cost - 100,000 * $0.0072 = $720 per hour.
            >> Daily cost (8 active hours) - $720 * 8 = $5760 per day.
            >> Monthly cost (~30days) - $5760 * 30 = $172,800 per month.

        >> Assumes efficient code and bulk coretime pricing.
        >> No real-time physics, just logic state updates at a manageable tick rate.
        >> Read-time combat and NPC AI simplified to tick-based events.
        >> No heavy state writes per tick - Only deltas and important changes.
        >> Players must accept slight latency due to tick-based model.
        >> Suitable for GTA-lite gameplay, but not continuous frame-by-frame simulation.
            >> Missions.
            >> Inventory.
            >> Movement.
            >> Combat logic. ^yBBkeBF3

**JZA ^sPHUeOQb

Blockchains with 600,000+ TPS (transactions per second) and asynchronous consensus unlock entirely new classes of algorithms and applications that are not practical—or outright impossible—on current blockchains limited by low throughput and sequential (synchronous) execution. ^iLjcexmK

Algorithms and Applications Newly Feasible with High-TPS Async Blockchains ^B2pBHcKF

Real-Time, Massive-Scale Coordination: ^6sQkpR4P

Real-time global applications like city-scale IoT management (controlling millions of devices), instant payroll for thousands/millions of users, and rapid, fair voting systems with instant finality become realistic because transactions aren’t queued or delayed by consensus bottlenecks. ^jBRYcCb1

Decentralized AI Model Training & Serving: ^TQ8WMrih

Federated or collaborative machine learning and AI inference across thousands of nodes, with dynamic model updates, rewards, and payments in real time—existing blockchains can’t keep up with the bandwidth or latency requirements for continuous, trustless model training. Asynchronous, high-TPS blockchains make this practical. ^8zw7bZyQ

Dynamic Supply Chains & Automated Market Makers: ^2RWlZqjH

Supply chains where sensors, oracles, and automated contracts update inventories, facilitate micro-transactions, and optimize routing in milliseconds. High TPS supports thousands of updates per second and async execution enables continuous, non-blocking state changes. ^Aa8QUayF

Batch and Parallel Processing Algorithms: ^kn35934e

Current blockchains must execute most transactions one after another due to consensus needs; high-TPS async chains can process thousands of unrelated (or conflict-free) operations in parallel, such as sharded state storage, large data crunching, and multi-user games. ^BEw5MnHt

Decentralized High-Frequency Finance: ^fbCdDADp

On-chain order books, slippage-free payments, and arbitrage become possible at speeds matching centralized exchanges, including microsecond-level settlement for billions of trades/day. This scale and speed are unfeasible on Ethereum or Bitcoin. ^FN8pi1yG

Composite Smart Contracts & Instant Cross-Chain Operations: ^U06fJ1wd

Contracts that interact with dozens of protocols simultaneously, or even run logic “across” blockchains atomically, can operate in real time—whereas in slow blockchains, these get stuck on sequencing and finality issues. ^FWdySYTy

Collaborative “Swarm” Algorithms: ^tFP8wS7s

Algorithms requiring the coordination of thousands of agents (IoT fleets, autonomous vehicles, distributed sensor networks) for collective action, which need sub-second responsiveness and trustless decision-making, are now possible. ^ENAkiJES

Why These Are Not Feasible Today ^rFlxThmL

Present blockchains (like Bitcoin, Ethereum) are blocked by slow, sequential execution—often a few dozen TPS—so complex, real-time, or “many-at-once” algorithms hit performance walls or become too expensive to run. ^PYfXOmrt

Synchronous consensus means all network nodes must agree in lockstep, so operations are limited by the slowest participants and by network latency. ^56hfA9u8

In Summary ^QhTPrefz

High-throughput, asynchronous blockchains like JAM are expected to unlock real-time, large-scale, and collaborative algorithms—including decentralized AI, large IoT control, instant on-chain finance, and highly parallel automation—that simply cannot function on today’s slow, sequential blockchains.Algorithms that require ultra-high-frequency, real-time processing and mass parallelism are not practical on today’s blockchains, but become feasible with an async, 600k+ TPS blockchain like JAM. ^Lb79DOjK

Examples include: ^Fj52RWps

Real-time microtransaction streams: Massively parallel, per-second payments (for IoT, content, cloud compute, or pay-per-use platforms) are impossible at low TPS and high latency. ^MH6cPcr3

City-scale IoT/automation: Decentralized orchestration of millions of sensors, devices, and vehicles—with rules and triggers evaluated every second for all—can only run if the chain can ingest, process, and settle updates at city scale instantly. ^7WQQo2u2

Collaborative/Swarm Algorithms: Algorithms for dynamic resource allocation, robot swarms, and highly adaptive supply chains, where hundreds of agents need to continually synchronize state, decision-making, and monetary transfers in <1s cycles. ^3h7udb5F

On-chain federated learning: Training and updating large-scale AI/ML networks with thousands of on-chain participants reporting, voting, and being paid in real time, which stalls or gets priced out on low-TPS sync chains. ^R3NXzCNl

Instant global voting or lotteries for millions of users, with results and fraud prevention determined in seconds rather than hours or days. ^zPQsQVrX

High-frequency on-chain DeFi: Market makers, arbitrageurs, or dynamic pricing models matching CEX/TradFi speed, with stateful updates hundreds of times a second. ^elc5sXUj

Why these aren’t possible today: ^FF7qlCcc

Legacy chains (Bitcoin/Ethereum) require sequential execution and have strict TPS caps, making parallel, real-time, or multi-agent algorithms prohibitively slow, expensive, and often outright impossible to run at global scale. ^pdGoHqnv

Async, high-TPS blockchains remove these limits, enabling new mathematics, economic, gaming, robotics, and collaborative AI algorithms that simply can’t run otherwise ^0xHngQyw

Algorithms that can be used for DAOs and decentralized systems to be able to make more autonomous decisions. ^EIKd4Bpu

With a high-TPS, asynchronous blockchain like JAM, DAOs and decentralized systems can employ advanced algorithms that dramatically expand their autonomy and decision-making capabilities—far beyond what’s feasible on legacy low-TPS, synchronous blockchains. ^yaSqd2IK

Autonomous DAO Algorithms Enabled by High-TPS Async Blockchains ^WTlnzzPD

Continuous & Real-Time On-Chain Governance: ^lhTN6DlL

DAOs can implement dynamic, streaming governance—where thousands of members continuously adjust rules, budgets, and proposals in real time, rather than batching votes over hours or days. Adaptive weighting and real-time decision markets become possible, letting DAOs dynamically tune governance based on active participation and feedback. ^pHT5lUqH

Decentralized “Swarm Intelligence” Algorithms: ^HQrsgXxA

Algorithms from robotics and decentralized AI, such as real-time collaborative sensing, automated bidding, or supply chain optimization, can be directly encoded into DAOs. These allow autonomous coordination of hundreds or thousands of agents (sub-DAOs, IoT devices, contributor pools) for collective resource allocation, problem-solving, and negotiation. ^o5v0T0tK

Algorithmic Treasury & Resource Management: ^VDjtzKry

DAOs can use fast, parallel financial algorithms to automate spending, investments, and grant allocation using deterministic or AI-driven portfolio balancing—reacting instantly to market or event triggers across thousands of transactions. ^BaxLfgtV

Decentralized Arbitration & Dispute Resolution: ^vaFOhFWb

High-throughput, async blockchains enable on-chain mediation where millions of users can propose, review, and decide on disputes collectively, even integrating AI-powered “jury selection” or pattern recognition for fairness and speed. ^pXfOufbQ

Smart Contract Composability & Orchestration: ^4lPJx2LG

DAOs can schedule and execute complex, multi-step workflows—e.g., phased project releases, collaborative competitions, or multi-DAO integrations—in parallel and asynchronously, making autonomous, large-scale coordination practical for the first time. ^fHrS82Wo

Adaptive Delegation & Liquid Democracy: ^HWv7kvKq

Advanced models like liquid democracy and automated delegation (where voting power smoothly shifts in real-time to trusted representatives) are feasible at mass scale, increasing both flexibility and the likelihood of informed, autonomous decision-making. ^Tixvb2qo

Algorithmic Identity & Reputation Systems: ^3NovzQjc

With asynchronous processing and massive TPS, DAOs can use real-time, cross-platform identity and reputation scoring to automate membership, rewards, and punishment systems continually as members interact. ^jpNND787

Why These Weren’t Feasible Before ^aIcJY7CM

Low TPS and synchronous consensus forced DAOs to batch votes, limit transaction complexity, and avoid real-time response models due to congestion and high fees. ^umEFqhjp

Async, high-TPS chains like JAM let DAOs automate, parallelize, and accelerate processes, result in self-governing, adaptive, and resilient organizations. ^SyIhVlMb

Examples ^QYA3mcHM

Real-time budgeting for organizations with thousands of contributors. ^sqoWAKBN

Decentralized grant competitions where dozens of projects receive live feedback and scoring. ^vYGwR5gG

Autonomous algorithmic responses to on-chain events—such as community responses, fraud detection, or security triggers—without manual input. ^nhiXD9TG

In summary, JAM’s scale and architecture transforms DAOs from simple voting clubs into fully autonomous, collaborative, and adaptive digital organizations capable of making, managing, and executing decisions in real time and at massive scale.High-TPS and asynchronous blockchains like JAM allow DAOs to deploy more complex, scalable, and autonomous decision-making algorithms that legacy blockchains cannot support. Examples include: ^CiK9AJ5s

Continuous Real-Time Governance: DAOs can shift from periodic batch voting to always-on, adaptive decision flows. Members and delegates can change votes, funding, and parameters on-the-fly, fine-tuning governance without the delays or congestion seen in current systems. ^gSYom9vL

Swarm Decision-Making & Agent Coordination: Algorithms from robotics and decentralized AI—such as collective negotiation, resource allocation, and collaborative sensing—can be natively integrated, enabling thousands of DAO agents, contributors, or sub-groups to coordinate and reach consensus in seconds. ^2dn18DSE

Dynamic Treasury & Financial Management: DAOs can automate treasury allocations, investments, and grants instantaneously with on-chain analytics, real-time scoring of proposals, AI-driven budgeting strategies, and threshold-based triggers for autonomous action. ^aROqggis

Automated Arbitration & Conflict Resolution: DAOs can operate large-scale, decentralized dispute resolution processes (millions of participants) with instant, algorithmic verdicts—using pattern recognition, machine learning, or majority consensus at a scale not possible with old blockchains. ^HyRsUZ4K

Liquid Democracy & Real-Time Delegation: Models allowing instant reallocation of voting power to delegates or experts, continuously optimizing governance and engagement for more representative and autonomous decisions. ^4tWpLow9

Autonomous Proposal Filtering, Prioritization & Funding: DAOs can run real-time scoring, ranking, and filtering of thousands of proposals and fund disbursement events autonomously as new information arrives, a challenge for slow chains. ^2K0Kt0K2

Such algorithms were impractical before due to network congestion, long block times, and sequential processing—on JAM, DAOs can operate as true digital organizations with parallel, adaptive, and self-governing logic. ^WaqZwtbm

Unsudo JAM SDK / Host ^OOS9td6g

>> A collection of tools and a service that hosts agents.
>> Allows developers to write high level agents/actors that do useful things for whatever purpose they have.
>> Has a package manager just like crate.io but strictly for   ^NvSmgHpt

use ::unsudo::prelude::*;

synth!(
    name: "Hello World",
    description: "I do something simple",
    version: "1.0.0",
    pkgs: [
        "udo-20"
    ],

    new {
        log "Hello World Synth initialized!";
        schedule Tick 10;
        
        # cross service calls
        call@polkadot("Astar", ...);
        
        # cross synth calls
        call@synth(...);

        // in built access to the internet
        GET "/youtube/"

        POST "/"
    }

    on Tick {
        print "Hello World"
    }

    

    on_timer(ctx, label) {
        if label == "tick" {
            ctx.log("Tick...");
            ctx.schedule_timer("tick", 10);
        }
    },

    on_message(ctx, msg) {
        ctx.log(format!("Received message: {:?}", msg));
    }
); ^x40PtmJX

Synth ^CmkWklC4

>> A high-level autonomous program running on the Unsudo protocol.
    >> React to events like messages, timers, and initialization.
    >> May react to Unsudo level events such are external http or rpc calls from outside the decentralized virtual machine.
    >> May schedule recurring or set time to perform some operation without any user actively triggering it.
    >> Manages its own state and behavior over time.
    >> Interacts seamlessly with Unsudo's APIs, other synths, and polkadot's ecosystem.

>> Better and faster than smart contracts.
>> Different and focused on more ergonomic API than JAM CorePlay and other parachains.
    >> Once product market fit is discovered, the API will be tailored to that purpose.
    >> Should be able to serve websites, videos, and data in a reasonably ergonomic way.
    >> Should be able to run large language models and complex systems.
    >> No size limit : ) - time to ditch 24kb and 4mb limits for application size.
    >> No pay per transaction, not pay per time or compute, simple staking model (people stake $UDO or $DOT) which generates compute passively. ^C5gGFX80

/*
Vote outcome enum
*/
enum Outcome {
    Defaulted,
    Rejected,
    InFavour,
    Against,
    Abstained
}

impl Default for Outcome {
    fn default() -> Self {
        Outcome::Defaulted
    }
}

/*
Voter data structure to be held inside the proposal
*/
#[derive(Default)]
struct Voter {
    wallet_address: WalletAddress,
    vote: Outcome
}

/*
Standard ethereum address = 42 bytes
 */
type WalletAddress = reliq::array::Array<0x2A, u8>;

/*
 Unix timestamp
*/
type UnixTimestamp = i32;

/*
    First 16 bytes of a sha-256 hash
*/
type Sha256Slice = u128;

/*
    Two byte long data structure, first byte holds the value between 0-100, second byte holds a single decimal place
*/
type Percentage = u16;

/*
Exported functions by the proposal smart contract
*/
pub trait ProposalTrait {
    fn vote(&mut self, voter: WalletAddress, vote: Outcome) -> Outcome;
    fn get_vote(&self, voter: WalletAddress) -> Outcome;

    fn proponent(&self) -> WalletAddress;
    
    fn opening_date(&self) -> UnixTimestamp;
    fn closing_date(&self) -> UnixTimestamp;

    fn proposal_pass_contract(&self) -> WalletAddress;
    fn proposal_fail_contract(&self) -> WalletAddress;

    fn voters(&self) -> Vec<Voter>;

    fn description(&self) -> String;

    fn encoded_hash(&self) -> Sha256Slice;

    fn new() -> Self;

    fn get_votes_for(&self, vote_type: Outcome) -> u128;    

    
}

/*
Proposal data structure
*/
pub struct Proposal {
    magic: i16,
    version: i16,
    blockchain_address: WalletAddress,
    proponent: WalletAddress,
    opening_date: UnixTimestamp,
    closing_date: UnixTimestamp,
    minimal_participation_percentage: Percentage,
    minimal_approval_percentage: Percentage,
    pass_callback: WalletAddress,
    fail_callback: WalletAddress,
    voters: Vec<Voter>,
    description: String,
    hash: Sha256Slice
} ^Vu8SMzYI

Capitalize on JAM increased speed and do more or offer more features onchain. ^qdtzCQJN

Core Features of JAM:

- Scalability: Targets over 1,000,000 transactions per second via elastic scaling, asynchronous backing, and parallel message processing.

- Agile Coretime: Lets developers and DAOs buy computational resources “on demand” and resell them, rather than locking them in slots—improving efficiency and reducing costs.

- Interoperability: Streamlines cross-chain messaging and eliminates previous technical limits on transferring information between chains; XCMP is now fully integrated.

- Modularity & Permissionlessness: Projects (like DAOs) won’t need to win expensive auctions to deploy; service chains and smart contracts deploy with fewer hurdles.

- Developer Incentives: A major allocation of DOT and KSM is earmarked to encourage teams to develop on the new tech stack.

- Gasless Model: Introduces a gasless (or very low cost) transaction paradigm, particularly for application-centric operations.

- Backward Compatibility: Existing parachains can migrate without changes, ensuring continuity for current projects.

 ^u6aPFfbv

Comparison: User Experience in DAOs ^DoaOQx4N

Joining a DAO: ^QYgr9iXV

JAM DAOs let users join immediately—no need for special tokens or technical procedures. ^QM9Wo7t5

Ethereum DAOs often require buying ETH and paying gas fees, which can be a barrier for new users. ^UTAMgu6K

Pre-JAM Polkadot DAOs were limited by technical parachain restrictions and could require more steps. ^F7E9BC7s

Participation/Voting: ^mw294Yfd

JAM supports “gasless” or very low-cost voting and proposals; users can interact instantly and see results quickly. ^IcvDfzL2

On Ethereum, every vote or proposal needs gas payment and can face network delays. ^VpObazUK

Legacy Polkadot governance was faster than Ethereum, but still less seamless than JAM’s model. ^ZTEgQpaD

Cost and Speed: ^ZY9AQ9P1

JAM DAOs almost eliminate participation costs, even for frequent or large-scale actions. ^Z9NyyX7i

Ethereum’s costs fluctuate, and transactions may take several minutes, especially when busy. ^FDd06q2G

JAM dramatically scales activity—DAOs can run large events (e.g., big votes, payroll) instantly and cheaply. ^EPIwsWDo

Features and Interconnectivity: ^gaFCJwPK

JAM DAOs naturally talk to other DAOs and blockchains, allowing users to participate in multi-community projects and processes from a single interface. ^EZAtBbwM

On other chains, these integrations typically require complex apps or “bridges” that add overhead and delay. ^uEBoDafM

Who Uses DAOs? ^Iwsa19Kl

Individuals: ^ujbWDqJ1

Crypto enthusiasts seeking democratic governance and transparency. ^tmNOrBVy

Creators and artists—who build community-driven funding, curation, and ownership platforms. ^HDnOhY3x

DeFi users—who want to participate in protocol upgrades, treasury allocation, and yield strategies. ^Po7mrEOZ

Activists—who coordinate for nonprofits, climate efforts, or social impact at scale. ^nK1dAzis

Businesses: ^njs1LInQ

Web3 startups use DAOs for transparent decisions, product development, and incentive programs. ^K1Tpe3jc

Larger enterprises explore DAOs for supply chain transparency, insurance, digital asset management, and collaborative research. ^Jd2NJ7Gj

Platforms (e.g., gaming, content creation) build DAOs to drive player/creator participation, revenue sharing, and moderation. ^JZDTYsgC

What Pain Points Do DAOs Solve? ^Q1QDO59C

Centralized Control: Replaces hierarchy and single-controller risks with code-based, collective governance. ^ZTSmCE9t

Transparency: All votes, transactions, and funding decisions are visible on-chain, reducing fraud and opacity. ^U4tHzGHl

Participation: Enables global, borderless engagement and ownership—anyone with a token can propose changes or vote without intermediaries. ^ijYXZ5Lm

Automation: Smart contracts handle tasks like payments, grants, and audits, lowering admin costs and error rates. ^2xCodKW7

Speed: DAOs facilitate rapid decision-making (compared to slow corporate governance), but often hit technical bottlenecks on legacy chains. ^cVUC4ljt

Common DAO Pain Points (Legacy Blockchains) ^DuSi2us7

High costs per transaction (gas fees) ^dqapL0Fl

Congestion delays: slow voting or proposal execution ^qfgTPch7

Low voter turnout and engagement (“voter apathy”) ^2JbCFFK8

Overcentralization: large token holders (“whales”) dominate votes ^TyuYldif

Complexity: users struggle with wallets, bridges, and difficult interfaces ^wGJnFccm

How JAM DAOs Solve These Issues ^oCYlkDM4

Cost: JAM eliminates or dramatically lowers gas fees, so users and businesses can participate for nearly free. ^WZyPnIDz

Scalability: JAM handles millions of transactions per second—large DAOs, payroll, or supply chains can operate in real time without delays or congestion. ^tH9fJ8BJ

Participation: On JAM, DAOs can easily send notifications, leverage mobile voting, and integrate cross-chain collaborations, making participation easier and more engaging. ^aWa5kfwx

Decentralization: JAM’s architecture lets DAOs launch and grow permissionlessly—no expensive slot auctions or centralized obstacles. ^0t8Gy5Ab

Composability: JAM DAOs can plug into other networks, apps, or DAOs natively, unlocking new business models (shared treasuries, joint ventures, global payroll, supply chain). ^sDGt3nyT

User Experience: JAM simplifies onboarding—users join, vote, and participate with much less technical friction, making DAOs accessible to non-experts and larger businesses. ^076gge1S

Most Benefited Industries ^9paiVdJJ

Finance & DeFi: ^GNr2G9j4

JAM’s scalability, low costs, and composability make it ideal for decentralized lending, asset management, and automated investment platforms. Real-time governance and liquidity management become practical for global DeFi networks. ^WPBkHyjH

Gaming & Esports: ^mXb8Rawb

DAOs in JAM enable rapid, cheap in-game transactions, collective moderation, and prize pools. Game studios, guilds, and competitive gaming organizations can instantly coordinate tournaments, player votes, and community-driven content. ^yvBHW9CH

Art & Content Creation: ^4r3B3NoV

Creative platforms, NFT marketplaces, and artist collectives use JAM DAOs for collaborative curation, royalty distribution, and cross-platform voting. Large communities and multi-asset projects become possible without technical bottlenecks. ^DBNQRDiF

Enterprise Automation: ^WZwXIHaw

Businesses in supply chain, payroll, procurement, or R&D automate complex multi-party coordination. JAM’s gasless, modular services lower costs and improve efficiency for multinational operations and workflow management. ^tauN3sJc

Civic, Advocacy & Non-Profit: ^dqQTKkl9

DAOs built on JAM enable fast, inclusive voting for cities, NGOs, charities, and advocacy groups. Large fundraising, budgeting, and decision-making tasks can be coordinated globally, increasing engagement and transparency. ^6uR4YFrM

Real Estate & Local Governance: ^ABpvdIFF

Homeowner associations, cooperatives, and local councils adopt DAOs for property management, budgeting, and elections, taking advantage of JAM’s low overhead and instant communications. ^2ow8i6p2

Research & Development Collaboratives: ^8wD40oSo

Scientific and tech alliance groups use JAM DAOs for grant allocation, open sourcing, and cross-border project management, leveraging composability and automation to accelerate innovation. ^NGWSwyiR
